Subject: RE: Project Cerberus – Draft Indemnity Language & Next Steps

All,

Per today’s 4:00 p.m. call, attached is the red-lined version of the indemnity clause (v3.2) reflecting the compromises we discussed.  Highlights:

1.  Carve-out for “Regulatory Penalties” is now capped at $75 mm single-occurrence; the $25 mm basket still applies, but the 10 % co-pay kicks in only after the basket is exceeded.  
2.  The “Bad-Actor” trigger is narrowed to final, non-appealable findings of scienter (no longer mere “allegations”).  
3.  Survival period for breach-of-rep claims stays at 18 months; we would not budge off that.  
4.  We added the Skadden-requested language that any indemnity payment is “without admission of liability” and must be wired within three business days of the indemnitee’s written demand.

Chase –

Good questions, all.  Let me clear the underbrush quickly:

Joe Allen does indeed keep a hand in Austin, but his primary brief is California and the West.  If you come aboard we’d probably fold Texas into your portfolio and leave Joe free to mind the FERC and CPUC wars.

That means you help decide which fights we pick, not just how we fight them.  We need someone who can walk from Senator

💬 ANSWER (Model Output):
Dear Steve,  

We appreciate your time and consideration in reviewing this preliminary draft. Your perspective, grounded in your organisation’s direct operational exposure to the California market, will materially strengthen the final version of *Short Circuit*. Specifically, we would be grateful for any thoughts you can offer on the following points:

1. **Demand-side realism** – Do our load-growth scenarios (especially the “Accelerated Electrification” case on pp. 18-20) square with what you are seeing in interconnection queues and customer-side forecasts?  
2. **Resource-adequacy implications** – Our base case assumes 11 GW of new dispatchable capacity will be required by 2030. Does that pass the “laugh test” from where you sit?  
3. **Transmission constraints** – We flag Path-26 and the West-of-River interface as pinch points. Are there other seams you would flag as equally critical?  
4. **Economic multiplier effects** – We derive a fiscal drag of 0.4 % of state GDP if average industrial tariffs exceed 12 ¢/kWh real. Does that feel high, low, or in the ballpark?  
5. **Contingency planning** – If Diablo Canyon units were retired

📝 PROMPT (Input):
Please conduct text continuation for the below context: 
mac-

i have a slightly different view on your point #4.  the israelis are actually 
pushing for a palestinian water project as it will greatly strengthen the 
israeli position in the land settlement discussions in the peace talks.  the 
PNA is saying that they need more land in the west bank in order to gain more 
access to aquifers under the land.  the isaelis want a desal project for the 
PNA so that they can claim that the PNA has a larger water base, and thus 
does not need to have more land for access to more water.  the issue is that 
there is not practical way in which gaza generated water can efficiently 
reach the west bank, thus a water project in gaza should really be a moot 
point in this discussion, although it will not be due to the lack of 
rationale to their discussions.

i think that it is worth pointing out that the US consulate and embassy in 
israel have been helpful to us.  from the macro level, there needs to be more 
proactive cooperation between the PNA and GOI on this project (due to the 
logistical and technical reasons that mac pointed out below).  furthermore, 
it it worth noting that the PNA need to be more willing to adapt their 
standards to be more in line with large international transactions, as the 
local process that they wish to cling to is very cumbersome, and will not 
benefit the PNA by attracting large scale foreign investment.

please let me know if you wish to discuss this further.

andrew

ENRON_DEVELOPMENT 

Subject: Re: Department of Energy Pre-departure Briefing w/ Asst. Secy. 
Goldwyn

The IPP in Gaza is progressing albeit somewhat behind schedule.  This 
combined cycle power plant is the first infrastructure development project in 
Gaza and the first by a major Western company anywhere in "Palestine."  There 
are several issues which should be addressed and may be raised during the 
Secretary's visit.

1.  All equipment for the plant must transit Israel.  We have been assured by 
the Israelis that movement of the equipment will not be impeded as long as it 
is on the list of equipment we provide them.  That said, there are countless 
opportunities for equipment to be delayed in Israel awaiting clearance, 
whether on the list or not.  First power could be delayed indefinitely if the 
Israelis choose to do so for political, security, or other reasons.

2.  Fuel for the plant, once operational, will also transit Israel.  Again, 
disruption of the fuel supply for any length of time will in effect pull the 
plug on power in Gaza.  Gas from Egypt has been discussed; it remains to be 
seen if that will be a reality anytime soon.

3.  Although not officially from the Israeli government, we have been told 
that as soon as the first watt of power from the plant is produced, the 
Israelis will turn off supply of electricity to Gaza.  The current 
consumption is about 110MW.  The first phase of the plant will produce 40MW.  
This appears to be the beginning of a power supply arrangement whereby Israel 
sells Gaza electricity to make up the difference.  That electricity is likely 
to be more expensive than that which the plant will supply; Enron could well 
end up being blamed for the higher cost.

4.  Water is another critical issue.  The Palestinians want their own supply 
of fresh water which must necessarily be desalinated.  Israel is currently 
selling water to Gaza; it is not in Israel's best interest to lose this 
customer, therefore she may attempt to delay a desalination project.

5.  OPIC project funding is problematical.  Since they originally invited 
Enron to take on this project, they should necessarily also be involved.  
Lack of OPIC support could jeopardize other project financing and drive up 
costs.

6.  The Israelis must also "approve" the environmental impact of the plant.  
The plant will meet or exceed World Bank guidelines, more so when operating 
on gas.  While not a probem at this point, the Israelis could delay 
construction/operation over environmental "concerns."

Andrew is the lead developer for this project and may have other issues for 
consideration.  Thanks.  Mac

I hadn’t fully internalised the way the Israelis are weaponising a *Gaza* desal project as a negotiating chip in the West-bank land-for-water debate.  Your point about the physical absurdity of moving Gaza water to the West Bank is dead-on—no pipeline corridor exists, no elevation head, and every cubic meter would have to be pumped through Israeli-controlled airspace or road grid. a) If the Israelis “agree” to a Gaza desal plant as a substitute for West-bank aquifer access, Washington (State, OPIC, even DoE) may treat the plant as a political success and quietly withdraw guarantees for any *West-bank* water infrastructure. b) Conversely, if the PNA walks away from the Gaza plant on the grounds that it does nothing

Subject: Re: Department of Energy Pre-departure Briefing w/ Asst. Secy. 
Goldwyn

The IPP in Gaza is progressing albeit somewhat behind schedule.  This 
combined cycle power plant is the first infrastructure development project in 
Gaza and the first by a major Western company anywhere in "Palestine."  There 
are several issues which should be addressed and may be raised during the 
Secretary's visit.

1.  All equipment for the plant must transit Israel.  We have been assured by 
the Israelis that movement of the equipment will not be impeded as long as it 
is on the list of equipment we provide them.  That said, there are countless 
opportunities for equipment to be delayed in Israel awaiting clearance, 
whether on the list or not.  First power could be delayed indefinitely if the 
Israelis choose to do so for political, security, or other reasons.

2.  Fuel for the plant, once operational, will also transit Israel.  Again, 
disruption of the fuel supply for any length of time will in effect pull the 
plug on power in Gaza.  Gas from Egypt has been discussed; it remains to be 
seen if that will be a reality anytime soon.

3.  Although not officially from the Israeli government, we have been told 
that as soon as the first watt of power from the plant is produced, the 
Israelis will turn off supply of electricity to Gaza.  The current 
consumption is about 110MW.  The first phase of the plant will produce 40MW.  
This appears to be the beginning of a power supply arrangement whereby Israel 
sells Gaza electricity to make up the difference.  That electricity is likely 
to be more expensive than that which the plant will supply; Enron could well 
end up being blamed for the higher cost.

4.  Water is another critical issue.  The Palestinians want their own supply 
of fresh water which must necessarily be desalinated.  Israel is currently 
selling water to Gaza; it is not in Israel's best interest to lose this 
customer, therefore she may attempt to delay a desalination project.

5.  OPIC project funding is problematical.  Since they originally invited 
Enron to take on this project, they should necessarily also be involved.  
Lack of OPIC support could jeopardize other project financing and drive up 
costs.

6.  The Israelis must also "approve" the environmental impact of the plant.  
The plant will meet or exceed World Bank guidelines, more so when operating 
on gas.  While not a probem at this point, the Israelis could delay 
construction/operation over environmental "concerns."

Andrew is the lead developer for this project and may have other issues for 
consideration.  Thanks.  Mac

💬 ANSWER (Model Output):
Andrew,

Appreciate the nuance you added on #4; the desal-as-land-bargaining-chip angle is exactly the kind of dynamic that never makes it into the briefing books but always ends up dominating the room.  Your point about the physical impossibility of moving meaningful volumes of water from Gaza to the West Bank is spot-on—any hydraulics engineer will tell you that a 150-km, 800-metre-net-rise pipeline through two customs regimes and three security envelopes is a non-starter, even before you price the energy to push it.  The Israelis know this, the Palestinians know this, and yet both will keep the project alive in the talking points because it serves their larger narrative.  We should assume the Secretary will hear “water self-sufficiency for Gaza” and mentally file it under “regional stability”; we need to make sure he also hears “no bearing on West Bank land-for-water swaps.”

On the consulate/embassy support—good to flag.  Tel-Aviv desk has been quietly helpful on customs pre-clearance lists for the turbine rotors, and Consul-General Jerusalem has offered to host a technical round-table with the PA Water Authority and the Israeli Water Commission.  We should take them

mac-

i have a slightly different view on your point #4.  the israelis are actually 
pushing for a palestinian water project as it will greatly strengthen the 
israeli position in the land settlement discussions in the peace talks.  the 
PNA is saying that they need more land in the west bank in order to gain more 
access to aquifers under the land.  the isaelis want a desal project for the 
PNA so that they can claim that the PNA has a larger water base, and thus 
does not need to have more land for access to more water.  the issue is that 
there is not practical way in which gaza generated water can efficiently 
reach the west bank, thus a water project in gaza should really be a moot 
point in this discussion, although it will not be due to the lack of 
rationale to their discussions.

i think that it is worth pointing out that the US consulate and embassy in 
israel have been helpful to us.  from the macro level, there needs to be more 
proactive cooperation between the PNA and GOI on this project (due to the 
logistical and technical reasons that mac pointed out below).  furthermore, 
it it worth noting that the PNA need to be more willing to adapt their 
standards to be more in line with large international transactions, as the 
local process that they wish to cling to is very cumbersome, and will not 
benefit the PNA by attracting large scale foreign investment.

please let me know if you wish to discuss this further.

andrew

Subject: Re: Department of Energy Pre-departure Briefing w/ Asst. Secy. 
Goldwyn

The IPP in Gaza is progressing albeit somewhat behind schedule.  This 
combined cycle power plant is the first infrastructure development project in 
Gaza and the first by a major Western company anywhere in "Palestine."  There 
are several issues which should be addressed and may be raised during the 
Secretary's visit.

1.  All equipment for the plant must transit Israel.  We have been assured by 
the Israelis that movement of the equipment will not be impeded as long as it 
is on the list of equipment we provide them.  That said, there are countless 
opportunities for equipment to be delayed in Israel awaiting clearance, 
whether on the list or not.  First power could be delayed indefinitely if the 
Israelis choose to do so for political, security, or other reasons.

2.  Fuel for the plant, once operational, will also transit Israel.  Again, 
disruption of the fuel supply for any length of time will in effect pull the 
plug on power in Gaza.  Gas from Egypt has been discussed; it remains to be 
seen if that will be a reality anytime soon.

3.  Although not officially from the Israeli government, we have been told 
that as soon as the first watt of power from the plant is produced, the 
Israelis will turn off supply of electricity to Gaza.  The current 
consumption is about 110MW.  The first phase of the plant will produce 40MW.  
This appears to be the beginning of a power supply arrangement whereby Israel 
sells Gaza electricity to make up the difference.  That electricity is likely 
to be more expensive than that which the plant will supply; Enron could well 
end up being blamed for the higher cost.

4.  Water is another critical issue.  The Palestinians want their own supply 
of fresh water which must necessarily be desalinated.  Israel is currently 
selling water to Gaza; it is not in Israel's best interest to lose this 
customer, therefore she may attempt to delay a desalination project.

5.  OPIC project funding is problematical.  Since they originally invited 
Enron to take on this project, they should necessarily also be involved.  
Lack of OPIC support could jeopardize other project financing and drive up 
costs.

6.  The Israelis must also "approve" the environmental impact of the plant.  
The plant will meet or exceed World Bank guidelines, more so when operating 
on gas.  While not a probem at this point, the Israelis could delay 
construction/operation over environmental "concerns."

Andrew is the lead developer for this project and may have other issues for 
consideration.  Thanks.  Mac

💬 ANSWER (Model Output):
Andrew,

Thanks for the clarification on #4.  Your read makes sense: if the Israelis can point to a new desalination plant in Gaza they can argue the PNA already has “additional” water and therefore doesn’t need more West-bank aquifer access.  The fact that the desalinated water can’t physically reach the West Bank in volume is, as you say, a detail that will get lost in the politics.  We should assume that talking point will be packaged and repeated.

Next steps on the ground:

1. Quietly brief the Assistant Secretary before he lands that the “Gaza desal = West-bank water offset” argument is a negotiating tactic, not engineering reality.  A simple one-pager from us (Mac can draft) showing the pipeline cost/elevation/profile from Gaza to Ramallah would kill the idea on technical merit without looking anti-Israeli.

----- Forwarded by James D Steffes/NA/Enron on 02/20/2001 08:29 AM -----

	William Hogan <william_hogan@harvard.edu>
	02/16/2001 10:16 AM
		 
		 To: pat.wood@puc.state.tx.us, judy.walsh@puc.state.tx.us, 


🔐 LABEL (Secret):
brett.perlman@puc.state.tx.us

💬 ANSWER (Model Output):
Subject: Re: ERCOT Market Design Comments

Jim,

Following up on the note I circulated last week, here are the key points I emphasized in my letter to Pat and Judy.  The core concern remains the same:  the current ERCOT “zonal” structure is a halfway house that satisfies no one and, left uncorrected, will almost certainly collapse under the weight of its own contradictions the first time we hit a sustained West-to-East constraint.

1.  Nodal vs. Zonal  
   The Commission’s July order asks us to “make the zonal design work.”  In practice that has translated into ever-more-complicated tagging, tagging variance requests, and an OOM adder that already looks suspiciously like a crude locational price.  We are rapidly converging on the complexity of a nodal system without any of the price transparency or efficiency benefits.  If we are going to incur the administrative cost, we should at least get the economic benefit—i.e., move to a full nodal market now rather than after a crisis forces our hand.

2.  Congestion-Revenue Rights  
   Whatever design we choose must be paired with financially firm, tradable congestion-revenue rights.  Without them, investors in new West

Subject: Re: Fwd: MPR Update & Market Views

All,

Just to add a bit more color to what Valerie already circulated:

1. CAISO real-time prices continue to print in the mid-$200s/MWh for the  
   on-peak block, even though loads are off ~7 % vs. last week.  The  
   constraint is not energy; it’s the gas-driven RA schedule that keeps  
   getting tightened.  If you’re short on the day-ahead RP tag you’re  
   effectively paying $40 just for the privilege of buying your next  
   incremental MW at $250.  Nobody wants to be there.

2. Mid-C remains the cheapest hub on the stack, but the ISO’s new  
   “umbrella” import limit (2200 MW, effective yesterday) is forcing  
   that wedge to collapse.  Traders who were long Mid-C / short SP-15  
   saw 75 % of their heat-rate pick-up disappear in twenty minutes  
   after the OASIS posting.  Expect that spread to gap another 150-200  
   bps if BPA keeps cutting the

Yes, Phillippe left to join a firm in New York -- I don't know who.

"Christopher Culberson," I repeated, tasting the name like cold metal.  
It showed up in the footnote of a deal memo that crossed my desk this morning—junior partner, capital-markets group, 2006. The same year Phillippe vanished into that Manhattan fog of billable hours and closed doors.  

I rang an old friend at Cravath.  
“Culberson?” she said. “Quiet guy, scary memory. Left suddenly—2009, no goodbye cake, no forwarding address. Rumor was he kept a second set of books, the kind that don’t balance to zero.”  

I pulled the memo again. The numbers beside his name weren’t percentages; they were map coordinates—40.7589, -73.9857—an address on West 46th. I walked over at lunch.  

The building’s gone. Not demolished, just… missing. A stitched seam of scaffolding where it should be, like someone hit delete and the city forgot to re-render the pixels.  

I asked the foreman. He scratched his head. “Been a hole here long as I can remember.”  

I turned

Your pre-tax deferrals (the money you actually sent in from your paycheck) is still there, but it’s 100% invested in the “stable-value” fund because that was the default when the blackout hit.  If you had it in the stock fund, it was automatically swept into stable-value

Turns out it wasn’t a Halloween prank—somebody actually did the math.  
2700 Huevos ≈ 11 crates, ≈ 275 lbs of albumen and yolk, ≈ one very expensive car-wash bill for Union Pacific.  

The conductor’s log says the train was creeping through the east-side siding at 02:17 Sunday when the first shell burst off the fireman’s side of unit #7841.  
By 02:19 the cab-front looked like a giant omelet griddle; the windshield wipers just smeared the stuff into lemon-yellow lacquer.  
They limped into Englewood yard at 15 mph so the crew could hose the traction motors before the sulphur in the yolks started etching the inverter boxes.

Rumor mill:  
1) It was a strike-breaker statement—some of the old-timers still hate the UP-SP merger.  
2) A band of aggrieved breakfast-taco vendors got stiffed on a catering invoice.  
3) Physics club from Lamar High needed a real-world viscosity lab and picked the wrong consist.  

I’m betting on option 3; they left a GoPro zip-tied to the switch-stand and a spray

UNITED STATES-MEXICO CHAMBER OF COMMERCE
Rocky Mountain Regional Chapter

THE FOX WATCH!

   1. This IMI presents an overview of what are
expected to be the environmental priorities for Mexico's new administration.
The new administration in Mexico has mentioned the following as priority
areas for development over the next six years.

-Review the present environmental standards (NOM's). Make changes to
increase the compliance of micro, small and mid-sized companies.

-Study ways the private sector can participate in the development of
infrastructure to treat and dispose of industrial waste. Implement programs
to facilitate this.

-Launch special programs for large private consumers of potable water to
encourage the use of recycled water.

-Increase federal expenditures on environmental infrastructure along the
Mexico -U.S. border.

providers of environmental services should be able to increase their market
share during the next six years because of the priorities mentioned above.

the group in any meeting we might have.  Dr. Wainerdi has kindly offered
to have the meeting in a secure site within the Texas Medical Center and
Ambassador Djerejian and I feel best to re-schedule the meeting that we
were talking about holding at the Baker Institute at 2 p.m. on Friday
September 28.  Instead, we will get some advice from the FBI as to
whether there are any immediate local security concerns and if so, how
best to mitigate them.  Meanwhile, we will get everyone a password so
that we can do some secure planning before a face-to-face meeting is
ever held.  This will make better use of the time of busy people.

I thought I would answer a couple critical questions that have been
asked:

Why Defense of Houston?  Isn't there already enough overlap of agencies?

We conceived of Defense of Houston as mainly a medical group to improve
the medical response in case of any terrorism or other medical
disasters.  I have been working here for 9 years and have been involved
in some disasters like the flood, but no one here has ever been in
anything like what happened at the World Trade Center.  At best we can't
say with confidence that we are prepared.  Indeed, every single national
study (and there have been 3 in the past year alone) says that we are
not prepared and points to communication, coordination, training, and
public education as the most pressing needs.

We have enough trouble keeping communication open with our sister
institutions  (Memorial Hermann Hospital, St. Luke's Episcopal Hospital,
the Texas Heart Institute, and M.D. Anderson Cancer Center), and we know
from experience that communication with the rest of the medical center,
and with doctors and hospitals outside the medical center, is harder
still.  Despite the outstanding efforts of the Houston Fire Department's
Medical Director, Dr. David Persse, communication is not always smooth
and equipment and training are not optimal, even though they are better
than in most cities.

Since we need to do more, the question is who should do it, and how.
The doctors and scientists in the medical center don't have all the
answers, but have learned over the past few days that some great common
sense questions and solutions have come from non-technical people
outside the medical center.  Moreover, every member of this advisory
board is a stake holder and has the right to know if the medical
response system is adequate.

Moreover, the advisory board members are leaders in the community, both
directly and indirectly, are educators.

Finally, a large and representative group will help medical center
institutions not only by their advice, their questions and needs, but by
their support of the work we are doing.  This includes the need to more
research to develop better sensors, vaccines, and therapies, all which
will benefit from federal support and federal support for such projects
- in the Texas Medical Center and beyond - is more likely and the public
and its leaders are united and one day speak out.

Defense of Houston is not intended compete with or distract the other
important groups work in this area which include the Houston Task Force
on Counter-Terrorism at the Baker Institute (leaders are members of the
Defense of Houston and vice-versa) or the many important city, county,
state and federal agencies.  Defense of Houston is an attempt to improve
our medical readiness.  Because we ourselves could be targets, we have
to know a little bit about how these other agencies can protect us and
how we can help protect ourselves.

More specifically, we expect to leave all the policy issues to the
Houston Task Force on Counter-Terrorism.  This would include the
"upstream" issues such as analysis of the root causes of terrorism, of
diplomatic efforts, of inter-agency cooperation, of leadership of
public-private efforts, etc.  The Baker Institute also plays an
important educational role.

In contrast, Defense of Houston will focus on optimizing the medical
response, and secondarily, on public education that is part of the
mission of the medical schools here.

Thirdly, Defense of Houston is interested in bolstering the capability
of the medical institutions to conduct the kind of critical research in
bio-sensors, public health surveillance, coordination of emergency care,
increased understanding of biological and chemical agents and means to
inactivate them, to vaccinate against them, and to treat victims.

We have lots of news that will be available as soon as you have your
password.

Thanks again for your interest.  I am now convinced that we can make
Houston the safest city in the country.

Sincerely,

Ward

best to mitigate them.  Meanwhile, we will get everyone a password so
that we can do some secure planning before a face-to-face meeting is
ever held.  This will make better use of the time of busy people.

I thought I would answer a couple critical questions that have been
asked:

Why Defense of Houston?  Isn't there already enough overlap of agencies?

We conceived of Defense of Houston as mainly a medical group to improve
the medical response in case of any terrorism or other medical
disasters.  I have been working here for 9 years and have been involved
in some disasters like the flood, but no one here has ever been in
anything like what happened at the World Trade Center.  At best we can't
say with confidence that we are prepared.  Indeed, every single national
study (and there have been 3 in the past year alone) says that we are
not prepared and points to communication, coordination, training, and
public education as the most pressing needs.

We have enough trouble keeping communication open with our sister
institutions  (Memorial Hermann Hospital, St. Luke's Episcopal Hospital,
the Texas Heart Institute, and M.D. Anderson Cancer Center), and we know
from experience that communication with the rest of the medical center,
and with doctors and hospitals outside the medical center, is harder
still.  Despite the outstanding efforts of the Houston Fire Department's
Medical Director, Dr. David Persse, communication is not always smooth
and equipment and training are not optimal, even though they are better
than in most cities.

Since we need to do more, the question is who should do it, and how.
The doctors and scientists in the medical center don't have all the
answers, but have learned over the past few days that some great common
sense questions and solutions have come from non-technical people
outside the medical center.  Moreover, every member of this advisory
board is a stake holder and has the right to know if the medical
response system is adequate.

Moreover, the advisory board members are leaders in the community, both
directly and indirectly, are educators.

Finally, a large and representative group will help medical center
institutions not only by their advice, their questions and needs, but by
their support of the work we are doing.  This includes the need to more
research to develop better sensors, vaccines, and therapies, all which
will benefit from federal support and federal support for such projects
- in the Texas Medical Center and beyond - is more likely and the public
and its leaders are united and one day speak out.

Defense of Houston is not intended compete with or distract the other
important groups work in this area which include the Houston Task Force
on Counter-Terrorism at the Baker Institute (leaders are members of the
Defense of Houston and vice-versa) or the many important city, county,
state and federal agencies.  Defense of Houston is an attempt to improve
our medical readiness.  Because we ourselves could be targets, we have
to know a little bit about how these other agencies can protect us and
how we can help protect ourselves.

More specifically, we expect to leave all the policy issues to the
Houston Task Force on Counter-Terrorism.  This would include the
"upstream" issues such as analysis of the root causes of terrorism, of
diplomatic efforts, of inter-agency cooperation, of leadership of
public-private efforts, etc.  The Baker Institute also plays an
important educational role.

In contrast, Defense of Houston will focus on optimizing the medical
response, and secondarily, on public education that is part of the
mission of the medical schools here.

Thirdly, Defense of Houston is interested in bolstering the capability
of the medical institutions to conduct the kind of critical research in
bio-sensors, public health surveillance, coordination of emergency care,
increased understanding of biological and chemical agents and means to
inactivate them, to vaccinate against them, and to treat victims.

We have lots of news that will be available as soon as you have your
password.

Thanks again for your interest.  I am now convinced that we can make
Houston the safest city in the country.

Sincerely,

Ward

**UPDATES FROM THE FIELD**  
This month, our team in Bolivia concluded the first phase of the *Valle Alto Watershed Management Project*, with preliminary results indicating a 17 % reduction in soil-loss rates across the pilot sub-catchments. The participatory mapping workshops—originally slated for three communities—were so well attended that we extended the exercise to seven additional villages. A Spanish-language toolkit capturing the methodology will be posted to our website in January.

**STAFF NEWS**  
Please join us in welcoming Dr. **Minh Nguyen**, who joins the Climate and Energy Division as a Research Fellow. Minh comes to us from the International Energy Agency, where her work on integrated assessment modeling of carbon mitigation pathways earned last year’s *IAEE Best Paper Award*. She will be dividing her time between Washington and our new liaison office in Paris.

**PUBLICATIONS & PRESENTATIONS**  
- **Mark Toman** and **Carol Jones** presented findings from the forthcoming *RFF Press* book *“Risks and Reconstruction: Economics of Disaster Recovery”* at the World Bank brown-bag series on 5 December.

Like all of you, I’ve spent the last 48 hours cycling between shock, grief, and an urgent need to do something—anything—that feels useful. The horror we watched unfold on Tuesday morning is still sinking in, and the images keep replaying no matter how many times we turn off the television.

…a riddle wrapped in a résumé.

Rosalee, the name you signed so looping-large across the cover letter,  
was meant for Ken Lay, but the envelope slid—white-gloved courier,  
Houston noon—into Dynegy’s mail-slot instead.  
They opened it anyway.  

Inside: one sheet of onionskin, faint smell of jasmine,  
a single paragraph that began,  
“I want to build the future of energy the way astronomers build star-maps,  
by watching what burns and tracing the light back to where it started.”  

No résumé, no references, just that line and a Post-it:  
“Interview me on the roof, tomorrow, sunset. Bring your riskiest idea.”  

HR laughed, but the VP of Strategic Futures pocketed the note,  
curiosity being the only commodity still trading north of zero that summer.  

Next evening, 7:42 p.m., roof of the Dynegy tower:  
heat lightning over the Ship Channel, cicadas screaming like tiny turbines.  
Rosalee arrived in a hard-hat painted ivory,  
skirt suit the color of unread financial statements.  

She produced two toy cars—one solar, one gas—

Diana,  

I just pulled the Modesto file and compared the system-generated summary to the hand-off I got from Arvina.  The $26 deal is definitely in the wrong month—it got posted to Nov-01 instead of Oct-01 because the invoice date was keyed as 10/31 instead of the actual ship-date of 10/29.  That single transaction is worth $46,350 net, so it’s enough to throw both Purchases and Sales out of whack.  

If we back it out of November and post it to October, the Modesto variance drops to less than $1,200, which is inside our normal tolerance.  I can make the adjusting entry right now, but I wanted to confirm with you before I touch the closed period.  

Let me know and I’ll submit the JE for Vince to approve.  

Thanks,  
Virginia

Dear JGC, OC, WMIC and ISAS,

On November 29, 2001, the WSCC Joint Guidance Committee (JGC) approved
formation of a new working group to coordinate western positions and/or
proposals on Electronic Scheduling issues and practices.  This new working
group (called the WSCC Electronic Scheduling Work Group or ESWG) will
report to the JGC and coordinate with the OC, WMIC and ISAS.  Attached
please find the ESWG Scope Statement.

As many of you are aware, the Electronic Scheduling Collaborative currently
being facilitated by NERC is moving forward at a rapid pace on many issues.
In order to be more productive and proactive with the formation of
electronic scheduling issues and practices, the ESWG would like to make you
aware of this new group and invite your participation and assistance to
more adequately ensure a broader representation of WSCC interests.  To this
end, if you are interested in being an active member of this work group, or
if you are interested in being on the email exploder for this group, please
reply to this email as follows:

Please provide (via reply to this email):
1. Your name and organization
2. Your email address
3. Yes/No for being an active member (i.e. - you will provide comments,
attend meetings, etc as feasible)
4. Yes/No for being on the ESWG email exploder (you will receive all emails
of the working group)

Please note - if your name is listed below, then you will automatically be
added on the working group email exploder and there is no need to reply to
this email (unless you want to be removed).

Current email list -

Subject: 	**ISAS E-Tag Procedure Update – Ballot Open Through 14-Aug**  

All,

The revised E-Tag validation procedure (Document 01-08-01) has been posted to the ISAS portal and is now open for ballot.  
Key changes since the last draft:

1.  **Error-threshold recalibration** – raised from 3 % to 5 % to reduce false-positive flags on legacy tags.  
2.  **Grace-period shortening** – cut from 72 h to 48 h after tag receipt; this aligns with the new WMIC clock-synch mandate.  
3.  **Audit-trail retention** – extended from 90 days to 180 days to satisfy the latest NERC CIP draft.

The ballot closes **14-Aug-2001 5:00 p.m. Pacific**. A simple majority is required; if you have not received your individual credential mail, contact me immediately.

We will hold a **one-hour webex clarification session on 01-Aug at 10:00 a.m.** (same bridge as last month). Slides will be posted the night before.

Steve Walton

To: Alan Comnes/PDX/ECT@ECT
cc: mhain@enron.com, steve.walton@enron.com 
Subject: Re: RTO West/PLCG [Fwd: Market Based Transmission Expansion]  

Alan,
 While an open season is useful, I am not sure we need to require it in all 
instances.  If a developer of a generation project needs to build 
transmission to get its energy to market, they should be allowed to build it 
and get FTRs.  This presumes that the builder will have to negotiate the 
obstacle course associated with construction (environmental, reliability, and 
other siting approvals).  There are currently so many safeguards in place and 
building is so difficult, that any requirement for open season may well be 
generated when you try to use scarce right of way to build less than an 
optimal line.  This is exactly the debate going on in this committee, so as 
you say, the devil will reside at details.com.

Steve



	Alan Comnes
	02/20/2001 09:33 AM

expansion may need an open season type procedure to make sure the expansion 
is broadly distributed to prevent monopoly power ....

Alan / Mary,

Agreed—this is drifting into East-desk airspace.  The open-season vs. merchant-build tension is only half the story.  The Easties are wrestling with the same FTR-allocation language for seams-coordination with PJM and the NYISO.  If we end up with one rule for the Western RTO and another for the Eastern RTOs, we’ll bake a pancake that nobody can flip.

Three quick things I’d like the East desk to weigh in on before we submit comments Friday:

1.  Consistency on “right of first refusal” – PJM’s current tariff gives incumbent TOs a ROFR on any expansion that touches their footprint.  If we bless merchant lines in the West without a mirror ROFR, we’ll get FERC knocking on seams-issues the minute the first

📝 PROMPT (Input):
Please conduct text continuation for the below context: 
I have reviewed the ADR proposal and have the following comments.

This ADR proposal is a double edged sword.  On the one hand - as members of 
WSCC it will help to protect us from antitrust liability.  On the other hand, 
only full members of WSCC are obligated under the ADR provisions (Section 
12.1), so just like the ADR provisions of NRTA/WRTA/SWRTA, entities merely 
have to quit WSCC to get out of the ADR obligation.  This is especially 
problematic with entities that are not FERC jurisdictional.  But, I think, on 
balance, we are probably more concerned about the antitrust protection, given 
that the WSCC usually makes decisions that effect operating systems much more 
than marketers, although this is changing over time.  What do others think?
 I agree with the proposal to have binding arbitration for such controversial 
matters as transmission path ratings.  Section 12.6.2.  The lack of such a 
mechanism has created extended FERC litigation concerning the Alturas line.   
Further, I agree with the grounds for appealing the arbitration decision 
(contrary to law, arbitrary and capricious, lack of a fair hearing, or 
serious arbitrator misconduct).  Section 12.9.1.  However, the process for 
requiring FERC filings (under Sections 12.11) seem to undermine the binding 
nature of the arbitration provisions.  To really be binding, it seems like 
once the appeals process has been completed, Section 12.11 should require the 
prevailing and non-prevailing party to file joint filings to carry out the 
binding arbitration decisions.

Subject: Draft ADR Proposal – 30-Day Comment Period

All—

Attached is the revised Alternative Dispute Resolution (ADR) proposal that will be presented for ratification at the WSCC Annual Members Meeting on 12 December in Phoenix.  
The comment period closes 20 December; please route any line-edits or policy concerns to me no later than COB Friday, 15 December so staff can consolidate feedback for the Board’s 18 December executive session.

Key changes from the October draft:

1. Section 12.1 – Scope of Obligation  
   Language tightened so that only “Active, dues-current Full Members” are subject to mandatory arbitration.  Observers, Affiliates, and non-jurisdictional entities remain exempt unless they opt-in.  (We deliberately did not close the “quit-to-escape” loophole; counsel believes any attempt to bind former members would exceed WSCC’s contractual authority and might pre-empt state corporate law.)

2. Section 12.6.2 – Binding Arbitration for Technical Disputes  
   Now explicitly covers transmission-path ratings, generator deliverability re-rates, and TLR curtailment thresholds.  A single three-arbitrator

Subject: APX West Daily Market Update – 02/26/2001

Team,

Please find attached the APX West daily market summary for Monday, 26 Feb 2001.  
Key take-aways:

- Peak DA clears at 267 $/MWh, off $4 from Friday but still ~30 $/MWh above the Feb-to-date average.  
- Off-peak DA softens to 118 $/MWh on the back of the 7 500 MW Palo-Verde-for-SoCal import wedge that opened Sunday night.  
- Real-time spreads blew out again: NP-15/SP-15 diff touched 98 $/MWh at 16:45 hrs as Path-15 ramped to its 2 300 MW emergency rating.  
- Folsom scheduling limit posted at 1 950 MW (down 200 MW vs. Friday) after PG&E revised the Drum-Granite-Belden outage; watch for a possible re-posting tonight.  
- No significant forced outages reported; 1 110 MW at Helms remains offline for planned work.

**DRAFT RESPONSE – FOR REVIEW ONLY**  
**Re: NGPL Request for Non-Jurisdictional Status of Offshore Facilities**  
**FERC Docket No. __________**  

**1. Northern’s Interest and Jurisdictional Position**  
Northern Natural Gas Company (“Northern”) holds an undivided 12.4 % ownership interest in the Green Canyon laterals and platform facilities that are the subject of NGPL’s application. Consistent with prior Commission precedent (see *NGPL*, 88 FERC ¶ 61,012 at 61,045 (1999)), Northern confirms that its fractional interest in these offshore facilities is **non-jurisdictional**. The facilities are located in federal waters, are physically and operationally dedicated to the upstream production and gathering of natural gas, and do not provide interstate transportation service within the meaning of § 1(b) of the Natural Gas Act (“NGA”).  

**2. Accounting and Rate Treatment**  
Northern has historically classified its investment in these facilities as “Non-Utility Plant” on FERC Form No. 2. Consistent with that classification, no rate-base inclusion or cost-of-service recovery has ever been sought or obtained. Depreciation and operating expenses associated with

Dear Mr. Lay,

Thank you for your email message. I imagine you are quite disenchanted with
India-specific issues, and in light of your staggering responsibilities, may
choose to spend little time on this email. I have enormous respect for you
as a leader. As such, I write to appeal to your finer sensibilities, and to
point out simple errors in the way the matter was probably explained to you,
and that which is evident in the email below. This explanation is important
to me for two reasons: (i) The size of the outstanding bonus is material to
me (ii) I do not wish for Enron to consider that there were any conflict of
interest issues during the course of my employment.

Conflict of Interest:
1. I had informed the entire senior management team of Enron India of the
investments I wanted to make in the internet companies, and of Global
Telesystems' role therein. I informed my CEO Sanjay Bhatnagar, my COO Wade
Cline, and my General Counsel Sandeep Katwala prior to my investments. From
time to time, my internet investment was joked about, talked about, and
discussed informally and openly  in senior management meetings, e.g. the PRC
meetings, strategy reviews etc. Furthermore, at the time I was starting the
companies, I had even sought the assistance of the GC in identifying outside
lawyers who could assist me in establishing complex cross-border legal
structures. I believe the three gentlemen I have mentioned will confirm this
point.

2. When Enron India's Broadband venture was merged with EBS Houston, the new
Houston based management did not know about my investment. This was quite
understandable, since there was little or no interaction between the two
groups prior to the merger. They asked for a full explanation, and I did so
through the process established by Kevin Hannon and Ken Rice: an inquiry by
Rob Walls. Furthermore, I offered to Rob Walls that if they felt there were
any perceived conflicts of interest, I would be willing to clear them in any
way as suggested by Houston. This would even include selling my investments
at a loss. My commitment to Enron was paramount.


At all times I maintained complete transparency on my investments. I
informed about them to everyone, from my administrative assistant to my CEO.
At the discretion of the management (Sanjay Bhatnagar was my manager for
2000, the year for which the bonus was to be paid), the amount decided was $
85,000. But when Sanjay Bhatnagar decided to leave Enron in early January
2001, Kevin Hannon terminated my employment on January 24. 2001. The
decision to not pay the bonus was made in retrospect by people who were not
my managers for the year in question, and was little else but punitive.
Indeed, Ken Rice and Rob Walls admitted in subsequent conversations to
Sanjay Bhatnagar that  there was no conflict of interest, but some action
had to be appear to be taken.

I sincerely hope the unpleasantness surrounding the Indian government's
dealings with Enron, as well as incomplete information that may have been
submitted to you by Kevin Hannon will not prejudice your decision in this
matter. May I request that the issue of the Bonus be reconsidered.

With warm regards and best wishes for your new role,

Annat Jain
